The psalmist expounds on the royal splendor the king showed when fighting his enemies.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 45:3–PSA 45:5

## Psalms 45:4 (id: 24549)

As God’s agent of truth, humility, and justice (see [72:1–2](https://ref.ly/Ps72:1-Ps72:2), [4](https://ref.ly/Ps72:4), [7](https://ref.ly/Ps72:7)), the king can carry out awe\-inspiring deeds ([65:5](https://ref.ly/Ps65:5); [96:4](https://ref.ly/Ps96:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 45:4

## Psalms 45:6 (id: 24551)

Justice characterizes God’s rule*,* as it should characterize the king’s administration.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 45:6

## Psalms 45:6–9 (id: 24550)

The king is like a god among humans; he is anointed by God, he has received a mighty throne, and he dispenses justice as he rules forever. • The writer to the Hebrews applies [45:6–7](https://ref.ly/Ps45:6-Ps45:7) to Jesus as he argues that the Son is greater than the angels ([Heb 1:8–9](https://ref.ly/Heb1:8-Heb1:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 45:6–PSA 45:9

## Psalms 45:8 (id: 24552)

Fine spices mixed with oil create distinctive perfumes. • The king’s palaces were wood paneled with ivory inlays (cp. [6:4](https://ref.ly/Ps6:4); [Amos 3:15](https://ref.ly/Amos3:15)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 45:8

## Psalms 45:9 (id: 24553)

Marriages often sealed diplomatic connections between kings; the women became part of the harem (see [1 Kgs 11:1–3](https://ref.ly/1Kgs11:1-1Kgs11:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 45:9

## Psalms 45:10–12 (id: 24543)

The bride is a representative of the king. Powerful people and nations try to gain her favor in order to get close to the king. This is a great honor, and she is encouraged to give herself fully to her new position.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 45:10–PSA 45:12

## Psalms 45:12 (id: 24544)

The princess of Tyre: Israel traded with Tyre, a powerful city\-state. The noblest woman of the city bears a wedding gift to her counterpart, the new queen, who represents an honored kingdom.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 45:12

## Psalms 46:1 (id: 24557)

Hebrew *according to alamoth* (“maidens”; see also study note on 1 Chr 15:20). In worship, a choir or a group of instrumentalists performed this hymn.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 46:1
* **Associated Articles:** 1 Chronicles 15:20 (ID: `13532@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Psalms 46:1–3 (id: 24555)

Even when faced with natural disasters, the citizens of Zion express no fear.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 46:1–PSA 46:3

## Psalms 46:1–11 (id: 24554)

This psalm of Zion celebrates God’s special presence in Jerusalem (see also [Pss 48](https://ref.ly/Ps48:1-Ps48:14), [76](https://ref.ly/Ps76:1-Ps76:12), [87](https://ref.ly/Ps87:1-Ps87:7), [122](https://ref.ly/Ps122:1-Ps122:9)), inviting confidence in the Lord because the citizens of Zion enjoy his protection.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 46:1–PSA 46:11

## Psalms 46:4 (id: 24559)

Like the streams flowing through Eden, this river brings the water of life, restoration, and healing ([36:8–9](https://ref.ly/Ps36:8-Ps36:9); see [Gen 2:10](https://ref.ly/Gen2:10); [Ezek 47](https://ref.ly/Ezek47:1-Ezek47:23); [Rev 22:2](https://ref.ly/Rev22:2)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 46:4

## Psalms 46:4–6 (id: 24558)

The strong city where God dwells protects its inhabitants. They enjoy the river flowing inside the city. This river contrasts with the turbulent waters (the powers of the nations) outside the city.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 46:4–PSA 46:6

## Psalms 46:5 (id: 24560)

From the very break of day: In the ancient Near East, enemies attacked cities during the day; even during the most dangerous times, the Lord will protect the place where his name dwells.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 46:5

## Psalms 46:6 (id: 24561)

People and nations live in chaos*,* characterized by opportunism ([83:2](https://ref.ly/Ps83:2)), busyness ([39:6](https://ref.ly/Ps39:6)), and noise. These people shake, totter, and crumble*,* unlike the city of God (cp. [15:5](https://ref.ly/Ps15:5)). In contrast, when the godly feel disturbed, they cry out for God’s help ([42:5](https://ref.ly/Ps42:5), [11](https://ref.ly/Ps42:11); [43:5](https://ref.ly/Ps43:5); [55:17](https://ref.ly/Ps55:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 46:6

## Psalms 46:7 (id: 24562)

This refrain reminds readers of the identity of the God of Zion. • among us: Hebrew ‘immanu*,* part of the name Immanuel, “God with us” ([Isa 7:14](https://ref.ly/Isa7:14); [8:8](https://ref.ly/Isa8:8); [Matt 1:23](https://ref.ly/Matt1:23)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 46:7

## Psalms 46:8 (id: 24564)

Israel saw the Lord’s glorious works in the wilderness ([107:24](https://ref.ly/Ps107:24); [111:2](https://ref.ly/Ps111:2)), but the wicked, who have no concern for them ([28:5](https://ref.ly/Ps28:5)), are left desolate ([73:19](https://ref.ly/Ps73:19)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 46:8

## Psalms 46:8–9 (id: 24563)

This invitation to trust in the Lord offers a perspective on God’s interactions with human powers.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 46:8–PSA 46:9

## Psalms 46:10 (id: 24556)

A message directly from the Lord confirms the authenticity of the psalmist’s vision (see also [12:5–6](https://ref.ly/Ps12:5-Ps12:6); [32:8](https://ref.ly/Ps32:8)). • know that I am God: The Lord will do what he promises ([Isa 45:3](https://ref.ly/Isa45:3); [49:23](https://ref.ly/Isa49:23); [60:16](https://ref.ly/Isa60:16)). • God will be exalted ([Ps 47](https://ref.ly/Ps47:1-Ps47:9); [Isa 6:1](https://ref.ly/Isa6:1)); he will judge the nations and rescue his people (see [Pss 57:11](https://ref.ly/Ps57:11); [89:13](https://ref.ly/Ps89:13); [108:5–6](https://ref.ly/Ps108:5-Ps108:6); [118:16](https://ref.ly/Ps118:16)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 46:10

## Psalms 47:1 (id: 24567)

People clap their hands in praise and recognition of marvelous deeds ([47:3–4](https://ref.ly/Ps47:3-Ps47:4); see [98:8](https://ref.ly/Ps98:8); [Isa 55:12](https://ref.ly/Isa55:12); cp. [Nah 3:19](https://ref.ly/Nah3:19)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 47:1

## Psalms 47:1–2 (id: 24565)

As the sovereign king over the nations, God requires praise.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 47:1–PSA 47:2

## Psalms 47:1–9 (id: 24566)

This psalm celebrates God’s kingship over all the earth; it tells of God’s victory in dealing with Israel and the nations. Both Israel ([47:3–4](https://ref.ly/Ps47:3-Ps47:4)) and the nations ([47:9](https://ref.ly/Ps47:9)) participate in God’s kingdom.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 47:1–PSA 47:9

## Psalms 47:3–4 (id: 24568)

The Lord made Israel victorious, especially under David (see [18:40–50](https://ref.ly/Ps18:40-Ps18:50)). God’s mighty deeds of redemption manifest his love for Israel.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 47:3–PSA 47:4

## Psalms 47:4 (id: 24569)

Jacob’s descendants: Jacob was the father of all Israelites ([Gen 32:28](https://ref.ly/Gen32:28)); the name Jacob often represents the nation of Israel. • God loves and commits himself to Jacob (see [Pss 78:68](https://ref.ly/Ps78:68); [127:2](https://ref.ly/Ps127:2); see [Rom 11:28](https://ref.ly/Rom11:28)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 47:4

## Psalms 47:5 (id: 24571)

trumpets: Or *rams’ horns* (see [81:3](https://ref.ly/Ps81:3)). At times, rams’ horns were sounded in celebration ([98:5–6](https://ref.ly/Ps98:5-Ps98:6); [2 Kgs 11:14](https://ref.ly/2Kgs11:14); [1 Chr 15:28](https://ref.ly/1Chr15:28)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 47:5

## Psalms 47:5–6 (id: 24570)

The psalmist portrays the Lord as returning to heaven, having accomplished his victories. For a portrayal of his descent, see [18:9–16](https://ref.ly/Ps18:9-Ps18:16).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 47:5–PSA 47:6

## Psalms 47:7 (id: 24572)

People from all the earth (see [96:1–3](https://ref.ly/Ps96:1-Ps96:3)) will worship God.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 47:7

## Psalms 48:1–14 (id: 24573)

This song of Zion (see also [Pss 46](https://ref.ly/Ps46:1-Ps46:11), [76](https://ref.ly/Ps76:1-Ps76:12), [87](https://ref.ly/Ps87:1-Ps87:7), [122](https://ref.ly/Ps122:1-Ps122:9)) explicitly views Jerusalem (Zion) as the city of the Great King where the godly find protection ([48:1–3](https://ref.ly/Ps48:1-Ps48:3)). God rules from Zion in faithfulness, righteousness, and justice, and he inspires his subjects with confidence and joy ([48:9–11](https://ref.ly/Ps48:9-Ps48:11)). They commit themselves to seeing the glory of Zion for themselves so that they can tell the next generation about it ([48:8](https://ref.ly/Ps48:8), [12–14](https://ref.ly/Ps48:12-Ps48:14)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 48:1–PSA 48:14

## Psalms 48:3 (id: 24576)

God dwells in Zion to protect it from all enemies (see [46:5](https://ref.ly/Ps46:5)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 48:3

## Psalms 48:7 (id: 24577)

The ships of Tarshish*,* built for long voyages, represent human accomplishment (see also [2 Chr 9:21](https://ref.ly/2Chr9:21); [Isa 2:16](https://ref.ly/Isa2:16)). Their destruction might be the event mentioned in [1 Kgs 22:48](https://ref.ly/1Kgs22:48).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 48:7

## Psalms 48:9–11 (id: 24578)

The psalmist joyfully celebrates the Lord’s presence in the midst of the community, reflecting on the faithfulness, righteousness, and justice of God’s rule.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 48:9–PSA 48:11

## Psalms 48:12–13 (id: 24574)

towers . . . fortified walls . . . citadels: These structures provide a visual tour of Zion’s strength.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 48:12–PSA 48:13

## Psalms 48:14 (id: 24575)

No one is like God ([35:10](https://ref.ly/Ps35:10); [71:19](https://ref.ly/Ps71:19); [86:8](https://ref.ly/Ps86:8); [89:6](https://ref.ly/Ps89:6), [8](https://ref.ly/Ps89:8); [113:5](https://ref.ly/Ps113:5); see [Isa 40:18](https://ref.ly/Isa40:18), [25](https://ref.ly/Isa40:25)). He is the shepherd King ([Ps 95:7](https://ref.ly/Ps95:7)) who will guide (see [5:8](https://ref.ly/Ps5:8); [23:2](https://ref.ly/Ps23:2)) the sheep of his flock ([77:20](https://ref.ly/Ps77:20)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 48:14

## Psalms 49:1–2 (id: 24580)

Anyone who will listen can gain wisdom ([Prov 1:5](https://ref.ly/Prov1:5), [8](https://ref.ly/Prov1:8), [33](https://ref.ly/Prov1:33)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 49:1–PSA 49:2

## Psalms 49:1–4 (id: 24581)

The wisdom teacher invites everyone to the hall of wisdom; he promises to instruct with a proverb and a riddle.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 49:1–PSA 49:4

## Psalms 49:1–20 (id: 24579)

In this wisdom psalm, a wise teacher warns against enemies, whom he portrays as living a rich lifestyle and caring only for themselves ([49:5–9](https://ref.ly/Ps49:5-Ps49:9)). They cannot keep anyone alive, least of all themselves, even in their memories ([49:10–12](https://ref.ly/Ps49:10-Ps49:12)). Like fattened animals, they are led to slaughter ([49:14–20](https://ref.ly/Ps49:14-Ps49:20)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 49:1–PSA 49:20

## Psalms 49:4 (id: 24585)

The teacher used both proverbs and riddles for instruction in wisdom (see [Prov 1:5–6](https://ref.ly/Prov1:5-Prov1:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 49:4

## Psalms 49:5–8 (id: 24586)

If enemies who live for material rewards cannot sustain themselves, how can they give life to others?

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 49:5–PSA 49:8

## Psalms 49:11–12 (id: 24582)

The teacher brings wisdom through irony. Everyone dies, and even those with estates named after them will be forgotten.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 49:11–PSA 49:12

## Psalms 49:13–15 (id: 24583)

The teachers of false wisdom cannot rescue themselves from death. Only God has that power.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 49:13–PSA 49:15

## Psalms 49:16–20 (id: 24584)

The wicked might have rich, luxurious lives, but they forget that they cannot avoid death.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 49:16–PSA 49:20

## Psalms 50:1–3 (id: 24598)

The whole universe must pay attention when God approaches to bring justice into the world.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 50:1–PSA 50:3

## Psalms 50:1–23 (id: 24597)

God appears as judge to remind the godly and the ungodly in turn that outward performance of sacrificial rituals and recitation of the law are worthless without thankfulness, repentance, and justice.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 50:1–PSA 50:23

## Psalms 50:4–6 (id: 24607)

The Lord is coming to bring righteousness. This hope encourages his faithful people*,* but those who have been unfaithful will not experience blessing when the heavens above and earth below testify against them (cp. [Deut 30:19](https://ref.ly/Deut30:19); [31:28](https://ref.ly/Deut31:28); [32:1](https://ref.ly/Deut32:1); [Isa 1:2](https://ref.ly/Isa1:2)). The Lord comes to restore order to the world ([Pss 5:8](https://ref.ly/Ps5:8); [22:31](https://ref.ly/Ps22:31); [33:5](https://ref.ly/Ps33:5); [40:9](https://ref.ly/Ps40:9); [50:6](https://ref.ly/Ps50:6); [71:16](https://ref.ly/Ps71:16); [97:6](https://ref.ly/Ps97:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 50:4–PSA 50:6

## Psalms 50:7–13 (id: 24608)

The psalmist delivers a message against those who regard the sacrificial rituals as more important than having a proper heart attitude.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 50:7–PSA 50:13

## Psalms 50:9 (id: 24609)

Unlike pagan gods, God does not need food; instead, he wants his people to serve him with a right heart ([50:14–15](https://ref.ly/Ps50:14-Ps50:15)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 50:9

## Psalms 50:10 (id: 24599)

I own the cattle on a thousand hills: God has no needs; he possesses everything.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 50:10

## Psalms 50:14 (id: 24600)

Thankfulness means looking beyond self\-interest to express gratitude for God’s blessings (see [51:15–17](https://ref.ly/Ps51:15-Ps51:17); [54:6](https://ref.ly/Ps54:6)). • True sacrifice requires offering one’s self to the glory of God ([50:23](https://ref.ly/Ps50:23); [Rom 12:1–2](https://ref.ly/Rom12:1-Rom12:2)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 50:14

## Psalms 50:15 (id: 24601)

God’s response to his people’s cry is to rescue them.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 50:15

## Psalms 50:16–21 (id: 24602)

This message is against the wicked, who only pretend to listen to God.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 50:16–PSA 50:21

## Psalms 50:17 (id: 24603)

my discipline . . . my words: The wicked may observe rituals, but they reject more important matters such as justice, mercy, and faithfulness (cp. [Matt 23:23–24](https://ref.ly/Matt23:23-Matt23:24)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 50:17

## Psalms 50:21 (id: 24604)

Because he is patient, God remained silent.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 50:21

## Psalms 50:22 (id: 24606)

God will tear the wicked apart like a lion tearing its prey ([Isa 5:29](https://ref.ly/Isa5:29); [31:4](https://ref.ly/Isa31:4); [Ezek 19:3](https://ref.ly/Ezek19:3); cp. [Pss 7:2](https://ref.ly/Ps7:2); [22:13](https://ref.ly/Ps22:13)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 50:22

## Psalms 50:22–23 (id: 24605)

The wicked will experience God’s justice, while the godly will see God’s everlasting salvation.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 50:22–PSA 50:23

## Psalms 51:1 (id: 24620)

A prayer for God’s mercy and kindness is part of the genre of lament. Sometimes the plea comes from the psalmist’s acknowledgment of his weakness and sin, as here (see also [6:2](https://ref.ly/Ps6:2); [9:13](https://ref.ly/Ps9:13); [25:16](https://ref.ly/Ps25:16); [31:9](https://ref.ly/Ps31:9); [41:4](https://ref.ly/Ps41:4), [10](https://ref.ly/Ps41:10)). At other times, it arises from the psalmist’s commitment to the Lord (see [26:11](https://ref.ly/Ps26:11); [27:7](https://ref.ly/Ps27:7); [119:29](https://ref.ly/Ps119:29), [58](https://ref.ly/Ps119:58), [132](https://ref.ly/Ps119:132)). In each case, God’s mercy brings an inner renewal.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 51:1

## Psalms 51:1–2 (id: 24611)

The repentant offender has nothing to offer God. He needs God’s favor, mercy, forgiveness, and blessing before he can experience renewal and restoration.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 51:1–PSA 51:2

## Psalms 51:1–19 (id: 24610)

This moving prayer for restoration asks for God’s favor, mercy, forgiveness, and cleansing. Out of a broken spirit, the psalmist confesses and accepts responsibility for his sin ([51:3–6](https://ref.ly/Ps51:3-Ps51:6)), then petitions God to remove his guilt and renew him inwardly ([51:7–12](https://ref.ly/Ps51:7-Ps51:12)). The psalmist then recommits himself to a lifestyle of wisdom and joy in the service of God and others ([51:13–17](https://ref.ly/Ps51:13-Ps51:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 51:1–PSA 51:19

## Psalms 51:title (id: 24627)

See [2 Sam 11:1–27](https://ref.ly/2Sam11:1-2Sam11:27). The text of the psalm contains no explicit reference to this event.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 51:1

## Psalms 51:1–65.13 (id: 24612)

These psalms of David share a common thread in their reflection on the experience of evil. In [Ps 51](https://ref.ly/Ps51:1-Ps51:19), the psalmist confesses the evil he has done and asks God’s forgiveness. [Psalms 52–64](https://ref.ly/Ps52:1-Ps64:10) lament specific evils that David experienced. A song of praise ([Ps 65](https://ref.ly/Ps65:1-Ps65:13)) brings David’s laments to an end.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 51:1–PSA 65:13

## Psalms 51:2 (id: 24621)

Wash me . . . Purify me: The psalmist prays for cleansing and release from the guilt of his sin ([51:7](https://ref.ly/Ps51:7); see [26:6](https://ref.ly/Ps26:6); [Isa 1:16](https://ref.ly/Isa1:16), [18](https://ref.ly/Isa1:18); [4:4](https://ref.ly/Isa4:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 51:2

## Psalms 51:3–6 (id: 24622)

The psalmist accepts the consequences of his sin against God. He knows that there is nothing good in him unless God renews his life.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 51:3–PSA 51:6

## Psalms 51:4 (id: 24623)

Against . . . you alone: This is hyperbole—the psalmist knows he also sinned against human beings ([2 Sam 11:2–4](https://ref.ly/2Sam11:2-2Sam11:4), [15–17](https://ref.ly/2Sam11:15-2Sam11:17)), but his offense against God is more important. He expects God to rebuke him and will accept whatever verdict God renders (see [Rom 3:4](https://ref.ly/Rom3:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 51:4

## Psalms 51:5 (id: 24624)

I was born a sinner: The psalmist is not making excuses but is acknowledging the depth of his sinfulness. From the time we are conceived*,* we all share in the human condition of sinfulness.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 51:5

## Psalms 51:7 (id: 24625)

Purify me from my sins (literally *Purify me with the hyssop branch*; see [Exod 12:22](https://ref.ly/Exod12:22); [Lev 14:4](https://ref.ly/Lev14:4); [John 19:29](https://ref.ly/John19:29); [Heb 9:19](https://ref.ly/Heb9:19)): The psalmist depends on God to purify and renew him (see [Isa 1:18](https://ref.ly/Isa1:18)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 51:7

## Psalms 51:8 (id: 24626)

Regaining joy is a gracious gift from God. • you have broken me: The psalmist’s whole being feels broken ([6:2](https://ref.ly/Ps6:2); cp. [34:20](https://ref.ly/Ps34:20)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 51:8

## Psalms 51:10 (id: 24614)

A loyal spirit yields the inner qualities of constancy and perseverance.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 51:10

## Psalms 51:10–12 (id: 24613)

God’s Spirit renews and recreates the human spirit.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 51:10–PSA 51:12

## Psalms 51:11 (id: 24615)

your Holy Spirit: Only the power of the Holy Spirit can change the human will to make it “loyal” ([51:10](https://ref.ly/Ps51:10)) and “willing to obey” ([51:12](https://ref.ly/Ps51:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 51:11

## Psalms 51:13 (id: 24616)

As he learns from his sin and its consequences, the psalmist wants to teach others.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 51:13

## Psalms 51:14 (id: 24617)

shedding blood: This idiom includes any injustice, not just homicide ([9:12](https://ref.ly/Ps9:12); [58:10](https://ref.ly/Ps58:10); see [2 Sam 11:14–26](https://ref.ly/2Sam11:14-2Sam11:26); cp. [Isa 1:15–17](https://ref.ly/Isa1:15-Isa1:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 51:14

## Psalms 51:18–19 (id: 24618)

rebuild the walls of Jerusalem: These verses were probably added as a prayer for national restoration after the Exile (see [Pss 42–43](https://ref.ly/Ps42:1-Ps43:5)); the entire community in exile identified with the spirit of the psalm.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 51:18–PSA 51:19

## Psalms 51:19 (id: 24619)

Sacrifices offered in the right spirit come from a heart that is right with God and with others (see [15:2–5](https://ref.ly/Ps15:2-Ps15:5); [24:3–6](https://ref.ly/Ps24:3-Ps24:6); [50:14](https://ref.ly/Ps50:14); [Matt 5:23–24](https://ref.ly/Matt5:23-Matt5:24)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 51:19

## Psalms 52:1 (id: 24630)

is a paradigm of evil.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 52:1

## Psalms 52:1–4 (id: 24628)

Liars foolishly deceive themselves by thinking that they can be heroes by their words. They believe themselves innocent of any charges of evildoing, though they love evil and cause destruction. Those who speak curses, sarcasm, bitterness, arrogant boasting, and deceptions ([31:18](https://ref.ly/Ps31:18); [34:13](https://ref.ly/Ps34:13); [59:7](https://ref.ly/Ps59:7), [12](https://ref.ly/Ps59:12); [120:2–3](https://ref.ly/Ps120:2-Ps120:3); [140:3](https://ref.ly/Ps140:3), [9](https://ref.ly/Ps140:9)) bring about chaos, alienation, and destruction ([Jas 3:5](https://ref.ly/Jas3:5)). Their sharp words cut like swords and daggers ([Pss 55:21](https://ref.ly/Ps55:21); [57:4](https://ref.ly/Ps57:4); [64:3](https://ref.ly/Ps64:3)), the fangs of a snake ([140:3](https://ref.ly/Ps140:3)), or a razor.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 52:1–PSA 52:4

## Psalms 52:1–9 (id: 24629)

Wicked people use words to destroy relationships and communities. They foolishly think that they can be heroes by their words ([52:1–4](https://ref.ly/Ps52:1-Ps52:4)), but their fate suddenly overtakes them ([52:5–7](https://ref.ly/Ps52:5-Ps52:7)). In contrast, the godly renew their commitment to the Lord ([52:8–9](https://ref.ly/Ps52:8-Ps52:9)) and find their future secure in him.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 52:1–PSA 52:9

## Psalms 52:3 (id: 24631)

Deceptive speech changes one’s character.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 52:3

## Psalms 52:5 (id: 24633)

God will strike the wicked down like a pagan altar, idol, or unclean vessel ([Exod 34:13](https://ref.ly/Exod34:13); [Lev 11:35](https://ref.ly/Lev11:35); [2 Kgs 10:27](https://ref.ly/2Kgs10:27)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 52:5

## Psalms 52:5–7 (id: 24632)

God’s judgment on the wicked will come suddenly and forcibly. The godly rejoice in God’s justice, not in the misfortunes of the wicked.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 52:5–PSA 52:7

## Psalms 52:6 (id: 24634)

In Hebrew, the verbs here translated see and be amazed sound almost alike.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 52:6

## Psalms 52:8–9 (id: 24635)

The psalmist commits himself to a life of trust in the Lord. • An olive tree lives a long time when cared for ([1:3](https://ref.ly/Ps1:3); [128:3](https://ref.ly/Ps128:3)). The psalmist desires to be close to the Lord, his Savior and the source of his blessing ([18:6](https://ref.ly/Ps18:6); [23:6](https://ref.ly/Ps23:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 52:8–PSA 52:9

## Psalms 53:1–3 (id: 24636)

Paul uses the Greek translation of this passage in his description of the depravity of all people ([Rom 3:10–12](https://ref.ly/Rom3:10-Rom3:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 53:1–PSA 53:3

## Psalms 53:1–6 (id: 24637)

This psalm parallels [Ps 14](https://ref.ly/Ps14:1-Ps14:7), differing only in verse 5\.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 53:1–PSA 53:6

## Psalms 53:5 (id: 24638)

scatter the bones: Israel’s enemies would suffer shameful defeat and lack the peaceful rest of a decent burial (cp.[141:7](https://ref.ly/Ps141:7); [2 Kgs 9:10](https://ref.ly/2Kgs9:10); [Jer 8:1–2](https://ref.ly/Jer8:1-Jer8:2); [Ezek 6:5](https://ref.ly/Ezek6:5)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 53:5

## Psalms 54:1 (id: 24640)

were bent on violence.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 54:1

## Psalms 54:1–7 (id: 24639)

In this individual lament, occasioned by violent and godless people ([54:3](https://ref.ly/Ps54:3)), the psalmist turns to God in prayer and maintains his focus on God’s help ([54:4](https://ref.ly/Ps54:4)). He prays for God to save him from evil and to judge his enemies ([54:5](https://ref.ly/Ps54:5), [7](https://ref.ly/Ps54:7)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 54:1–PSA 54:7

## Psalms 54:3 (id: 24641)

The strangers were alienated from the covenant community (see [109:11](https://ref.ly/Ps109:11); [Prov 5:10](https://ref.ly/Prov5:10)). • Proud and violent people hold no regard for God or for other people (see [Pss 35:11](https://ref.ly/Ps35:11), [25](https://ref.ly/Ps35:25); [86:14](https://ref.ly/Ps86:14); [Prov 11:16](https://ref.ly/Prov11:16)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 54:3

## Psalms 54:6 (id: 24643)

A voluntary offering is a sacrifice of praise. Sacrifices of praise are the kind of offering God most desires (see [Ps 50:14–15](https://ref.ly/Ps50:14-Ps50:15), [23](https://ref.ly/Ps50:23)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 54:6

## Psalms 54:6–7 (id: 24642)

In anticipation of God’s response, the mood of the psalm changes from gloom to thanksgiving.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 54:6–PSA 54:7

## Psalms 55:1–3 (id: 24645)

The occasion for this prayer is an attack by an enemy.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 55:1–PSA 55:3

## Psalms 55:1–23 (id: 24644)

The psalmist is faced with adversaries whose leader is a former friend ([55:12–14](https://ref.ly/Ps55:12-Ps55:14)), and he is overwhelmed with anguish. He turns to the Lord in prayer and commits himself to the Lord for redress ([55:22–23](https://ref.ly/Ps55:22-Ps55:23)). Jesus experienced similar suffering when his friend (Judas) betrayed him. He also experienced the answer to the psalmist’s prayer: (1\) by taking the place of the wicked and receiving their judgment ([Isa 53:7–9](https://ref.ly/Isa53:7-Isa53:9)), and (2\) by committing himself to the Father, who rescued ([Luke 23:46](https://ref.ly/Luke23:46)) and rewarded him ([Isa 53:10–12](https://ref.ly/Isa53:10-Isa53:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 55:1–PSA 55:23

## Psalms 55:4–8 (id: 24650)

In the experience of deep anguish, the psalmist confesses that his adversity has become unbearable and he seeks an escape.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 55:4–PSA 55:8

## Psalms 55:12–20 (id: 24646)

The psalmist’s enemy was once a friend; memories of that close relationship bring him pain (see [31:11](https://ref.ly/Ps31:11)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 55:12–PSA 55:20

## Psalms 55:19–21 (id: 24647)

God is good and faithful, but the psalmist’s enemies and so\-called friend are deceitful and wicked.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 55:19–PSA 55:21

## Psalms 55:22–23 (id: 24648)

The psalmist commits all his cares to the Lord.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 55:22–PSA 55:23

## Psalms 55:23 (id: 24649)

To die young means that one fails to experience God’s full blessing (cp. [Prov 3:2](https://ref.ly/Prov3:2), [16](https://ref.ly/Prov3:16)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 55:23

## Psalms 56:1 (id: 24654)

by Philistines concerned about his loyalties and brought before King Achish ([1 Sam 21:10–15](https://ref.ly/1Sam21:10-1Sam21:15)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 56:1

## Psalms 56:1–13 (id: 24651)

This individual lament moves from petition to confidence two times ([56:1–4](https://ref.ly/Ps56:1-Ps56:4), [5–13](https://ref.ly/Ps56:5-Ps56:13)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 56:1–PSA 56:13

## Psalms 56:4 (id: 24655)

mere mortals: Or *mere flesh,* in contrast to the immortal and infinitely more powerful God (see [Isa 2:22](https://ref.ly/Isa2:22)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 56:4

## Psalms 56:5–7 (id: 24656)

The psalmist specifies his charge against his attackers as he prays for their evil to cease.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 56:5–PSA 56:7

## Psalms 56:8 (id: 24657)

The psalmist makes a play on the Hebrew words translated sorrows (Hebrew nod) and bottle (Hebrew no’d).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 56:8

## Psalms 56:11 (id: 24652)

mere mortals (Hebrew ’adam): The psalm also uses two other words for man: ’enosh (“people,” [56:1](https://ref.ly/Ps56:1)) and basar (“mere mortals,” [56:4](https://ref.ly/Ps56:4)). All express the ideas of mortality and finitude.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 56:11

## Psalms 56:13 (id: 24653)

The light of God’s goodness and salvation will replace the darkness of death (see [18:25–29](https://ref.ly/Ps18:25-Ps18:29); [27:1](https://ref.ly/Ps27:1)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 56:13

## Psalms 57:1 (id: 24660)

shadow of your wings: As a bird protects its young, God protects the one who trusts in him (see [17:8](https://ref.ly/Ps17:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 57:1

## Psalms 57:1–11 (id: 24658)

The psalmist’s cry for mercy and protection quickly turns into an affirmation of trust and confidence in the Lord ([57:1–4](https://ref.ly/Ps57:1-Ps57:4)). When the wicked fall into their own traps ([57:6](https://ref.ly/Ps57:6)), the psalmist will praise the Lord ([57:7–10](https://ref.ly/Ps57:7-Ps57:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 57:1–PSA 57:11

## Psalms 57:title (id: 24666)

See [1 Sam 22:1](https://ref.ly/1Sam22:1) or [Ps 24:1–8](https://ref.ly/Ps24:1-Ps24:8).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 57:1

## Psalms 57:2 (id: 24661)

God Most High (Hebrew *Elohim\-Elyon*) is a generic title for God, who is more powerful than all real or imagined supernatural beings (see [91:1](https://ref.ly/Ps91:1); [97:9](https://ref.ly/Ps97:9); [Gen 14:18–22](https://ref.ly/Gen14:18-Gen14:22)). • who will fulfill his purpose: Or *who will end my \[trouble],* or *who deals kindly with me*.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 57:2

## Psalms 57:3–4 (id: 24662)

These verses are a prayer for rescue from powerful enemies. The metaphor of fierce lions describes human enemies; the psalmist feels unable to escape them without God’s intervention (see also [35:11–21](https://ref.ly/Ps35:11-Ps35:21); [124:6](https://ref.ly/Ps124:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 57:3–PSA 57:4

## Psalms 57:5 (id: 24663)

This verse is repeated in [57:11](https://ref.ly/Ps57:11) and [108:5](https://ref.ly/Ps108:5). • God’s glory—his manifest presence that excludes all evil ([8:1](https://ref.ly/Ps8:1); [19:1–4](https://ref.ly/Ps19:1-Ps19:4); [24:7](https://ref.ly/Ps24:7))—will fill the whole created order ([Isa 6:3](https://ref.ly/Isa6:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 57:5

## Psalms 57:7–8 (id: 24665)

The psalmist is confident that God will end the night of evil (cp. [56:13](https://ref.ly/Ps56:13)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 57:7–PSA 57:8

## Psalms 57:7–11 (id: 24664)

This section is repeated with a few modifications in [108:1–5](https://ref.ly/Ps108:1-Ps108:5).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 57:7–PSA 57:11

## Psalms 57:10–11 (id: 24659)

God’s glory extends to the whole universe, as will praise for his unfailing love and faithfulness.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 57:10–PSA 57:11

## Psalms 58:1 (id: 24670)

rulers (or *gods*): This designation was used for high officials and administrators of justice ([82:1](https://ref.ly/Ps82:1), [6](https://ref.ly/Ps82:6)). Elsewhere, it might refer to angelic creatures ([89:7](https://ref.ly/Ps89:7)) or to the gods of the nations ([82:1](https://ref.ly/Ps82:1); [Dan 11:36](https://ref.ly/Dan11:36)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 58:1

## Psalms 58:1–5 (id: 24668)

The administrators of justice were furthering their own interests rather than those of the needy. The absence of justice gradually resulted in a violent society.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 58:1–PSA 58:5

## Psalms 58:1–11 (id: 24667)

The administration of justice is a mark of good government. God expects nothing less from his people. The psalmist condemns Israel’s leaders for abusing their power, and he calls on the Lord to rain down his curses upon the wicked. The demonstration of divine justice will reassure the godly that God is just.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 58:1–PSA 58:11

## Psalms 58:3 (id: 24671)

All human beings are born sinners (see [51:5](https://ref.ly/Ps51:5)); however, whereas the wicked indulge their sinful nature, the godly fight against it ([Rom 7:19–23](https://ref.ly/Rom7:19-Rom7:23); [Jas 4:1–10](https://ref.ly/Jas4:1-Jas4:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 58:3

## Psalms 58:4 (id: 24672)

Venom here means poisonous speech (see [140:3](https://ref.ly/Ps140:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 58:4

## Psalms 58:6 (id: 24673)

The psalmist calls on God to break the destructive power of the wicked.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 58:6

## Psalms 58:8 (id: 24674)

When moving over dry ground, snails dry up.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 58:8

## Psalms 58:9–10 (id: 24675)

God’s judgment will come suddenly. The certainty of vindication brings joy to the godly.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 58:9–PSA 58:10

## Psalms 58:11 (id: 24669)

Vindication is the reward of those who live for God.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 58:11

## Psalms 59:1 (id: 24680)

See [1 Sam 19:11–18](https://ref.ly/1Sam19:11-1Sam19:18).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 59:1

## Psalms 59:1–2 (id: 24677)

Protect me (or *place me on* *high*): When surrounded by evil, the godly pray for God to set them apart for himself and protect them.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 59:1–PSA 59:2

## Psalms 59:1–17 (id: 24676)

The psalmist laments the power of enemies who conspire with other nations.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 59:1–PSA 59:17

## Psalms 59:4–5 (id: 24681)

The psalmist asks God to show the treacherous, deceptive, and wicked traitors no mercy (see [25:2](https://ref.ly/Ps25:2)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 59:4–PSA 59:5

## Psalms 59:6–8 (id: 24682)

The wicked threaten like vicious dogs*,* but the Lord responds with scorn.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 59:6–PSA 59:8

## Psalms 59:11–13 (id: 24678)

The psalmist prays that the wicked will be forced to endure the same agonizing pain they have caused.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 59:11–PSA 59:13

## Psalms 59:14–16 (id: 24679)

The wicked will go unsatisfied*,* but the psalmist will praise the Lord’s power and unfailing love.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 59:14–PSA 59:16

## Psalms 60:1 (id: 24693)

This psalm was taught to young people as part of their education. • regarding the time: See [2 Sam 8:13–14](https://ref.ly/2Sam8:13-2Sam8:14). • Aram\-naharaim and Aram\-zobah refer to the Arameans of northwest Mesopotamia and Syria (see study note on Gen 24:10). • The tune “Lily of the Testimony” is unknown today.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 60:1
* **Associated Articles:** Genesis 24:10 (ID: `12481@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Psalms 60:1–4 (id: 24692)

When God’s people experience his discipline, they affirm their loyalty and await God’s answer.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 60:1–PSA 60:4

## Psalms 60:1–12 (id: 24691)

The psalmist laments a national defeat and cries out to God for rescue.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 60:1–PSA 60:12

## Psalms 60:3 (id: 24694)

wine (Hebrew yayin) that sent us reeling: Yayin, the generic Hebrew term meaning “wine,” is often associated with drunkenness ([2 Sam 13:28](https://ref.ly/2Sam13:28); [Isa 5:11](https://ref.ly/Isa5:11); [28:7–8](https://ref.ly/Isa28:7-Isa28:8); cp. [Ps 104:15](https://ref.ly/Ps104:15); [Deut 14:26](https://ref.ly/Deut14:26); [Isa 55:1](https://ref.ly/Isa55:1)); it symbolized God’s judgment of the wicked ([Ps 75:8](https://ref.ly/Ps75:8); [Jer 51:7](https://ref.ly/Jer51:7)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 60:3

## Psalms 60:4 (id: 24695)

God raised a banner over his people to serve as a rallying point and as a source of pride and confidence. • The fear of God that leads to wisdom ([111:10](https://ref.ly/Ps111:10)) is not weakness; it provides the perspective and strength required to do what is right.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 60:4

## Psalms 60:5 (id: 24697)

The psalmist prays for rescue based on the special relationship between God and his people.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 60:5

## Psalms 60:5–12 (id: 24696)

This section is repeated verbatim in [108:6–13](https://ref.ly/Ps108:6-Ps108:13).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 60:5–PSA 60:12

## Psalms 60:6 (id: 24699)

Shechem and Succoth represent Israel’s heartland.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 60:6

## Psalms 60:6–8 (id: 24698)

The Lord responds with a message of promise. He shares his land with his people as spoils of war.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 60:6–PSA 60:8

## Psalms 60:9 (id: 24700)

Destroying a defeated invader’s capital (the fortified city) would help to ensure that they would not attack again. The capital of Edom was Bozrah, located to the southeast of Israel. Only God could grant victory.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 60:9

## Psalms 61:1–8 (id: 24702)

The lamenting psalmist petitions God to protect him and lead him into his presence ([61:4–5](https://ref.ly/Ps61:4-Ps61:5)). He prays for God to extend and protect the king’s rule and makes vows to praise God.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 61:1–PSA 61:8

## Psalms 61:1–63.11 (id: 24701)

In these royal prayers, the psalmist longs for God’s presence. All three psalms concern themselves with the king’s security.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 61:1–PSA 63:11

## Psalms 61:2 (id: 24703)

the ends of the earth: This expression has the connotation of a cry that comes from a place far away from God. • The psalmist’s heart is overwhelmed with deep anguish.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 61:2

## Psalms 61:4 (id: 24704)

The poet longs for God’s presence and seeks entrance into God’s home on earth for divine protection and care. To be received into God’s sanctuary (literally *tent*) is the greatest good (see [15:1](https://ref.ly/Ps15:1); [23:6](https://ref.ly/Ps23:6); [24:3–6](https://ref.ly/Ps24:3-Ps24:6)). The Hebrew word translated *sanctuary* recalls the Tabernacle and the tent that David built for the Ark ([1 Chr 15:1](https://ref.ly/1Chr15:1)). See also study note on John 1:14. • The shelter of your wings symbolizes God’s protection and care (see [Pss 9:9](https://ref.ly/Ps9:9); [17:8](https://ref.ly/Ps17:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 61:4
* **Associated Articles:** John 1:14 (ID: `18788@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Psalms 61:5 (id: 24705)

you have heard my vows: The psalmist’s vows might also be a part of ceremonial sacrifices ([66:13–15](https://ref.ly/Ps66:13-Ps66:15); [76:11](https://ref.ly/Ps76:11); [116:14](https://ref.ly/Ps116:14), [18](https://ref.ly/Ps116:18)). He praises and thanks God for his rescue. • The inheritance refers to the promise of the land (see [Ps 60](https://ref.ly/Ps60:1-Ps60:12)), the sacredness of the Temple, the joy of God’s presence ([119:111](https://ref.ly/Ps119:111)), or any similar reward ([16:6](https://ref.ly/Ps16:6); [37:9](https://ref.ly/Ps37:9), [11](https://ref.ly/Ps37:11), [18](https://ref.ly/Ps37:18), [22](https://ref.ly/Ps37:22), [29](https://ref.ly/Ps37:29), [34](https://ref.ly/Ps37:34); [127:3](https://ref.ly/Ps127:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 61:5

## Psalms 62:1 (id: 24709)

See [1 Chr 25:1](https://ref.ly/1Chr25:1); also in [Pss 39](https://ref.ly/Ps39:1-Ps39:13) and [77](https://ref.ly/Ps77:1-Ps77:20).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 62:1

## Psalms 62:1–12 (id: 24706)

This psalm expresses confidence in the king and offers prayer for him. The king rests in God despite his difficulties. Although his deceptive and powerful adversaries push hard against him, he remains undaunted. He encourages himself and his people to trust in God and to see their human adversaries from God’s perspective, where they appear frail, fleeting, and deceptive ([62:9](https://ref.ly/Ps62:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 62:1–PSA 62:12

## Psalms 62:3–4 (id: 24710)

The enemies desire to topple the king with lies and cunning.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 62:3–PSA 62:4

## Psalms 62:4 (id: 24711)

My high position refers to the royal throne.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 62:4

## Psalms 62:5–8 (id: 24712)

The psalmist encourages himself and others to place their hope in God.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 62:5–PSA 62:8

## Psalms 62:9 (id: 24714)

not what they appear to be: People often try to look stronger and better than they are.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 62:9

## Psalms 62:9–12 (id: 24713)

Unlike God, humans are transient beings. Even when they thrive, their prosperity is fleeting.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 62:9–PSA 62:12

## Psalms 62:10 (id: 24707)

The crime of extortion means using intimidation and threats to make others give up what is theirs, while stealing is taking what belongs to others.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 62:10

## Psalms 62:11–12 (id: 24708)

Strength and unfailing love remain in balance only in God. He cares for his people by ridding the world of evildoers (see [1:6](https://ref.ly/Ps1:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 62:11–PSA 62:12

## Psalms 63:1 (id: 24717)

Together, soul and whole body refer to a person’s entire being. • parched and weary land: When David was living in the desert, he also found himself in a spiritual wasteland, separated from God’s people and the sanctuary.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 63:1

## Psalms 63:1–11 (id: 24715)

The king longs for God’s presence so vividly that he eats, drinks, and sees God’s goodness. Though worn out and harassed by the wicked, the king sees God, and this animates him with praise. God becomes his life ([63:3](https://ref.ly/Ps63:3)), while the wicked perish ([63:9](https://ref.ly/Ps63:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 63:1–PSA 63:11

## Psalms 63:2 (id: 24718)

The psalmist remembers that he had found God to be present in his sanctuary (see [18:6](https://ref.ly/Ps18:6); [61:4](https://ref.ly/Ps61:4); [62:5](https://ref.ly/Ps62:5), [11–12](https://ref.ly/Ps62:11-Ps62:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 63:2

## Psalms 63:3 (id: 24719)

better than life itself: The highest quality of life is found in relationship with God (see [4:7](https://ref.ly/Ps4:7); [63:5](https://ref.ly/Ps63:5); [Prov 16:8](https://ref.ly/Prov16:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 63:3

## Psalms 63:6 (id: 24721)

Meditating on God requires focused awareness, concentration, thought, and reflection (see [1:2](https://ref.ly/Ps1:2)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 63:6

## Psalms 63:6–8 (id: 24720)

The psalmist reflects and meditates on God’s presence through the night. In the absence of light, God is the light. The quietness of the night and the longing for dawn provide time to focus on the true significance of life with the Lord.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 63:6–PSA 63:8

## Psalms 63:9 (id: 24722)

The wicked were plotting to destroy the psalmist’s life through deception and lies, but they would meet their end. • The depths of the earth refers to the place of the dead.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 63:9

## Psalms 63:10 (id: 24716)

Wild jackals are associated with desolation and abandonment ([Isa 13:20–22](https://ref.ly/Isa13:20-Isa13:22); [34:10–15](https://ref.ly/Isa34:10-Isa34:15); [Jer 9:11](https://ref.ly/Jer9:11); [49:33](https://ref.ly/Jer49:33); [51:37](https://ref.ly/Jer51:37); [Lam 5:18](https://ref.ly/Lam5:18); [Mal 1:3](https://ref.ly/Mal1:3)). The wicked will not receive honor in death; their bodies will be left to scavenging animals ([Eccl 6:3](https://ref.ly/Eccl6:3); [Jer 22:18–19](https://ref.ly/Jer22:18-Jer22:19); [36:29–30](https://ref.ly/Jer36:29-Jer36:30)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 63:10

## Psalms 64:1–6 (id: 24724)

These verses form a prayer for rescue from bold and powerful liars.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 64:1–PSA 64:6

## Psalms 64:1–10 (id: 24723)

This lament decries the destructive plots and arrogant attitude of the wicked ([64:1–6](https://ref.ly/Ps64:1-Ps64:6)). The psalmist turns to God in prayer and takes heart in God’s justice because whatever the wicked do will be undone by the righteous judge. The godly will rejoice and grow in wisdom as they reflect on God’s mighty acts.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 64:1–PSA 64:10

## Psalms 64:3 (id: 24725)

their tongues . . . bitter words: Liars use their speech to kill. This description might also refer to practicing magic.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 64:3

## Psalms 64:6 (id: 24726)

The enemies create a perfect plan to harm the innocent without getting caught. • Human beings will sink to unthinkable depths with evil words, acts, or plans ([83:3](https://ref.ly/Ps83:3); see [Jer 17:9](https://ref.ly/Jer17:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 64:6

## Psalms 64:7–10 (id: 24727)

What God does is righteous. God undoes what is wicked and pays the wicked back with what they planned for others.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 64:7–PSA 64:10

## Psalms 65:1 (id: 24731)

God alone deserves praise and is worthy to have vows made to him.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 65:1

## Psalms 65:1–3 (id: 24729)

Praise is the appropriate response to God’s majestic deeds in redemption ([65:3–5](https://ref.ly/Ps65:3-Ps65:5)) and in nature ([65:5–13](https://ref.ly/Ps65:5-Ps65:13)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 65:1–PSA 65:3

## Psalms 65:1–13 (id: 24728)

This psalm recounts the reasons why all people should praise and revere God for his almighty power and merciful care for his universe.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 65:1–PSA 65:13

## Psalms 65:2 (id: 24732)

All of us means all humans.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 65:2

## Psalms 65:3 (id: 24733)

you forgive: God graciously removes the guilt of sin (see [32:5](https://ref.ly/Ps32:5)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 65:3

## Psalms 65:4 (id: 24734)

those you choose to bring near: This includes people from Israel and from other nations. • Only those whom God sees as blameless live in his holy courts ([Pss 15](https://ref.ly/Ps15:1-Ps15:5) and [24](https://ref.ly/Ps24:1-Ps24:10)). • At the Temple*,* God’s earthly palace, all nations can gather to experience God’s holy presence. No structure can separate humans from God (see [Rev 21:22](https://ref.ly/Rev21:22)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 65:4

## Psalms 65:6–7 (id: 24735)

God brought order to the mountains*,* the raging oceans*,* and the pounding waves (see [Pss 95:4–5](https://ref.ly/Ps95:4-Ps95:5); [104:6–9](https://ref.ly/Ps104:6-Ps104:9)). • The psalmist compares the chaotic forces of nature with the rebellion of the nations (see [2:1–12](https://ref.ly/Ps2:1-Ps2:12); [33:6–11](https://ref.ly/Ps33:6-Ps33:11); [46:6](https://ref.ly/Ps46:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 65:6–PSA 65:7

## Psalms 65:8 (id: 24736)

All over the world ([Job 38:13](https://ref.ly/Job38:13); see [Ps 50:1–3](https://ref.ly/Ps50:1-Ps50:3)), people should stand in awe of the Lord.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 65:8

## Psalms 65:9 (id: 24738)

The river of God and all rivers demonstrate God’s victorious power and goodness through the order in nature and the regularity of the harvests (see [1:3](https://ref.ly/Ps1:3); [36:8](https://ref.ly/Ps36:8); [46:4](https://ref.ly/Ps46:4); [Ezek 47:6–12](https://ref.ly/Ezek47:6-Ezek47:12); [Zech 14:8](https://ref.ly/Zech14:8); [Rev 22:1](https://ref.ly/Rev22:1)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 65:9

## Psalms 65:9–13 (id: 24737)

The created order provides clear evidence of God’s power ([65:6–8](https://ref.ly/Ps65:6-Ps65:8)). He shows his love by maintaining nature. Everything praises the Lord as all the parts fit together harmoniously.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 65:9–PSA 65:13

## Psalms 65:13 (id: 24730)

Nature bursts out in praise of the Creator (see [79:13](https://ref.ly/Ps79:13); [96:11–13](https://ref.ly/Ps96:11-Ps96:13); [148:3–5](https://ref.ly/Ps148:3-Ps148:5), [7–12](https://ref.ly/Ps148:7-Ps148:12); [Isa 55:12–13](https://ref.ly/Isa55:12-Isa55:13)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 65:13

## Psalms 66:1–4 (id: 24740)

The psalmist exhorts nature to join in a mighty chorus to praise God’s glory, name, and power.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 66:1–PSA 66:4

## Psalms 66:1–20 (id: 24739)

This anonymous psalm includes two hymns ([66:1–12](https://ref.ly/Ps66:1-Ps66:12), [13–20](https://ref.ly/Ps66:13-Ps66:20)) that praise God for his redemption.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 66:1–PSA 66:20

## Psalms 66:2 (id: 24747)

The glory of his name represents the perfection of God’s character.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 66:2

## Psalms 66:3 (id: 24748)

God conquers his enemies (see [18:44](https://ref.ly/Ps18:44); [59:11](https://ref.ly/Ps59:11); [81:15](https://ref.ly/Ps81:15)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 66:3

## Psalms 66:5–7 (id: 24749)

The story of the Exodus and God’s victory over the sea evokes responses of astonishment ([66:5](https://ref.ly/Ps66:5)) and worship ([66:6](https://ref.ly/Ps66:6)). God works miracles . . . for people in order to redeem them for himself.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 66:5–PSA 66:7

## Psalms 66:7 (id: 24750)

God’s power brings redemption. The same word occurs in [65:6](https://ref.ly/Ps65:6) for God’s power in creation (see also [80:2](https://ref.ly/Ps80:2)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 66:7

## Psalms 66:8–10 (id: 24751)

The orderly lives of the redeemed reflect the order of God’s world. The Lord protects them from evil and purifies them.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 66:8–PSA 66:10

## Psalms 66:9 (id: 24752)

God guides his people into wisdom and away from folly ([17:5](https://ref.ly/Ps17:5); [37:31](https://ref.ly/Ps37:31); [121:3](https://ref.ly/Ps121:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 66:9

## Psalms 66:10 (id: 24741)

You have tested us, O God: As the silversmith heats silver to remove the dross, God’s discipline removes sin from his people (see [12:6](https://ref.ly/Ps12:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 66:10

## Psalms 66:11 (id: 24742)

God decrees and permits the many trying circumstances his people experience.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 66:11

## Psalms 66:12 (id: 24743)

Then you put a leader over us: Or *You made people ride over our heads.* This expression possibly refers to a foreign ruler who kept the Israelites in submission.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 66:12

## Psalms 66:15 (id: 24744)

Vows often included sacrifices of thanksgiving, including burnt offerings (see [20:3](https://ref.ly/Ps20:3); [40:6](https://ref.ly/Ps40:6); [50:8](https://ref.ly/Ps50:8); [51:19](https://ref.ly/Ps51:19)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 66:15

## Psalms 66:16–20 (id: 24745)

The private testimony in the conclusion of the psalm parallels the public praise of its opening ([66:1–4](https://ref.ly/Ps66:1-Ps66:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 66:16–PSA 66:20

## Psalms 66:18 (id: 24746)

Confession leads to restoration (see [32:5](https://ref.ly/Ps32:5)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 66:18

## Psalms 67:1–2 (id: 24753)

This prayer is based on the priestly blessing found in [Num 6:24–26](https://ref.ly/Num6:24-Num6:26). God’s goodness to Israel affects his reputation among the nations (see [Ps 96:1–3](https://ref.ly/Ps96:1-Ps96:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 67:1–PSA 67:2

## Psalms 67:1–7 (id: 24754)

In this anonymous prayer for God’s blessing, the psalmist addresses the knowledge and worship of God among the nations (cp. [64:9](https://ref.ly/Ps64:9); [65:8](https://ref.ly/Ps65:8)). The prayer requests that God’s name be kept holy and that his kingdom come (cp. [Matt 6:9–13](https://ref.ly/Matt6:9-Matt6:13)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 67:1–PSA 67:7

## Psalms 67:3 (id: 24755)

God’s international reputation leads to his praise among the nations (see [138:4](https://ref.ly/Ps138:4)), whose turmoil and rebelliousness will cease when they join Israel in praise of God. • One God rules over all, and the nations will give thanks for God’s just rule (see [67:4](https://ref.ly/Ps67:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 67:3

## Psalms 67:4 (id: 24756)

God brings the ultimate justice that human beings desire ([96:10](https://ref.ly/Ps96:10); [98:9](https://ref.ly/Ps98:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 67:4

## Psalms 67:6–7 (id: 24757)

Bountiful harvests are evidence of God’s blessing ([Gen 26:12](https://ref.ly/Gen26:12); [Isa 30:23](https://ref.ly/Isa30:23)). • all over the world: See [Pss 2:8](https://ref.ly/Ps2:8); [96:1–3](https://ref.ly/Ps96:1-Ps96:3). • All will fear him*,* joining with Israel to acknowledge the God of Israel.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 67:6–PSA 67:7

## Psalms 68:1–3 (id: 24759)

These verses form a prayer for God to rise up and scatter his enemies*,* as he did when Israel entered the Promised Land (cp. [Num 10:35–36](https://ref.ly/Num10:35-Num10:36); [Josh 6–12](https://ref.ly/Josh6:1-Josh12:24)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:1–PSA 68:3

## Psalms 68:1–35 (id: 24758)

This prayer for God’s victorious rule traces God’s march from Sinai to Zion ([68:7](https://ref.ly/Ps68:7), [16–18](https://ref.ly/Ps68:16-Ps68:18), [24](https://ref.ly/Ps68:24)). He rescued the nation of Israel from Egypt, guided it through the wilderness, brought it into the Promised Land, and established his kingdom. He is a caring and victorious God, whose nature is unchanging. Therefore, the godly hope and rejoice in the prospect of God’s universal dominion.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:1–PSA 68:35

## Psalms 68:2 (id: 24774)

Both smoke and wax represent a transitory existence ([22:12–18](https://ref.ly/Ps22:12-Ps22:18); [37:20](https://ref.ly/Ps37:20); [102:3](https://ref.ly/Ps102:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:2

## Psalms 68:3 (id: 24779)

The lot of the godly contrasts with that of the wicked (see [1:6](https://ref.ly/Ps1:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:3

## Psalms 68:4 (id: 24781)

him who rides the clouds: This is an ancient description of Baal, a Canaanite deity. The psalmist applies it to the Lord as the true “Rider of the Clouds” (see [68:33](https://ref.ly/Ps68:33); [104:3](https://ref.ly/Ps104:3)). God wields power over the clouds and the rain.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:4

## Psalms 68:4–6 (id: 24780)

The psalmist praises God’s powerful and compassionate reign.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:4–PSA 68:6

## Psalms 68:5 (id: 24782)

God, the Father of all marginalized and needy people, shows them compassion and protects their rights (see [10:14](https://ref.ly/Ps10:14), [18](https://ref.ly/Ps10:18); [69:33](https://ref.ly/Ps69:33); [82:3](https://ref.ly/Ps82:3)). • Like his dwelling*,* the Lord is holy. The supreme example of the holy God coming down to rescue humanity is Jesus Christ.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:5

## Psalms 68:6 (id: 24783)

God released Israel from slavery in Egypt ([Exod 20:2](https://ref.ly/Exod20:2); see [Ps 69:33](https://ref.ly/Ps69:33)), and he still sets the prisoners free. • The sun\-scorched desert represents alienation from God and separation from blessing.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:6

## Psalms 68:8–10 (id: 24784)

The earth trembled as in an earthquake (see [Exod 19:18](https://ref.ly/Exod19:18)). • the heavens poured down rain: Wells in the desert and rain showers in Canaan supplied an abundance of water.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:8–PSA 68:10

## Psalms 68:11 (id: 24760)

a great army (or *a host of women*) brings: The Hebrew suggests women singers caroling the good news of victory (see [68:25](https://ref.ly/Ps68:25); [Exod 15:20](https://ref.ly/Exod15:20)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:11

## Psalms 68:13 (id: 24761)

The seminomadic pastoral people who lived among the sheepfolds were surprised to receive silver and gold carvings from the battle.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:13

## Psalms 68:14 (id: 24762)

The Almighty (Hebrew Shaddai) is an ancient designation for God (see [91:1](https://ref.ly/Ps91:1); [Gen 17:1](https://ref.ly/Gen17:1)). • The identification of Mount Zalmon is uncertain; it was possibly a mountain in Bashan (see [Ps 68:15](https://ref.ly/Ps68:15)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:14

## Psalms 68:15 (id: 24763)

Bashan is a plateau northeast of Jerusalem and east of the Sea of Galilee that was known for its woods and pastureland (see [22:12](https://ref.ly/Ps22:12); [Isa 2:13](https://ref.ly/Isa2:13)). The mountains might have included Mount Hermon to the north of the plateau or Mount Zalmon, which might be Jebel ed\-Druze on the east.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:15

## Psalms 68:16 (id: 24764)

Why do you look with envy? Bashan is physically much more impressive than the mountains around Jerusalem, but it was not the home of the Lord. Only Mount Zion enjoyed the privilege of providing God’s dwelling place.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:16

## Psalms 68:17 (id: 24765)

Chariots*,* the ultimate weapon of the armed forces in the psalmist’s day, are an image of the Lord’s power to grant victory ([20:7](https://ref.ly/Ps20:7); [44:3](https://ref.ly/Ps44:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:17

## Psalms 68:18 (id: 24766)

When you ascended to the heights: The Most High established his reign victoriously in Zion. • a crowd of captives . . . gifts: This is an image of a victorious ruler leading the victory march after battle. Paul applied this image to Jesus Christ ([Eph 4:8–13](https://ref.ly/Eph4:8-Eph4:13)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:18

## Psalms 68:19 (id: 24768)

Like a caring father or a shepherd, God carries his people in his arms (see [55:22](https://ref.ly/Ps55:22); [68:5–6](https://ref.ly/Ps68:5-Ps68:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:19

## Psalms 68:19–20 (id: 24767)

The Divine Warrior cares for his people like a shepherd; the people praise him.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:19–PSA 68:20

## Psalms 68:21 (id: 24769)

God will smash the heads of his enemies: Cp. [Gen 3:15](https://ref.ly/Gen3:15).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:21

## Psalms 68:22 (id: 24770)

God will deal with all enemies*,* whether from high places (represented by Bashan) or low (the depths of the sea).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:22

## Psalms 68:24–27 (id: 24771)

A great company joins together in the pilgrimage to Zion.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:24–PSA 68:27

## Psalms 68:28–31 (id: 24772)

The people pray for God’s success and for victory over the enemy nations ([68:1–3](https://ref.ly/Ps68:1-Ps68:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:28–PSA 68:31

## Psalms 68:29 (id: 24773)

The conquered kings will bring gifts to the Lord.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:29

## Psalms 68:30 (id: 24775)

Hostile and rebellious people delight in warfare and insurrection (see [2:1–3](https://ref.ly/Ps2:1-Ps2:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:30

## Psalms 68:32 (id: 24777)

The term kingdoms of the earth includes all nations and power structures.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:32

## Psalms 68:32–35 (id: 24776)

The psalmist calls for the people to praise God, who is victorious over his enemies.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:32–PSA 68:35

## Psalms 68:34 (id: 24778)

When the Lord demonstrates his power*,* majesty*,* and strength against the nations, he strengthens his people (see [96:4–6](https://ref.ly/Ps96:4-Ps96:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 68:34

## Psalms 69:1 (id: 24791)

[Psalm 45](https://ref.ly/Ps45:1-Ps45:17) also notes this tune, but [Ps 45](https://ref.ly/Ps45:1-Ps45:17) celebrates the glory of kingship, whereas [Ps 69](https://ref.ly/Ps69:1-Ps69:36) is the cry of a tired king who feels abandoned by the Lord.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:1

## Psalms 69:1–2 (id: 24785)

The psalmist sees himself as almost drowning but still surviving chaotic forces and alienation from God (see [42:7](https://ref.ly/Ps42:7); [69:14](https://ref.ly/Ps69:14), [15](https://ref.ly/Ps69:15); [Isa 8:8](https://ref.ly/Isa8:8)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:1–PSA 69:2

## Psalms 69:1–36 (id: 24786)

In this individual lament, the poet expresses his vulnerability, humiliation, and overwhelming pain, asking God to vindicate him for the sake of the righteous.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:1–PSA 69:36

## Psalms 69:1–72.20 (id: 24787)

These last four psalms of Book Two contrast with the psalms describing God’s mighty acts in creation and in redemption, capped by God’s march to Zion ([Pss 65–68](https://ref.ly/Ps65:1-Ps68:35)). [Psalms 69–71](https://ref.ly/Ps69:1-Ps71:24) portray the king’s anguish, and [Ps 72](https://ref.ly/Ps72:1-Ps72:20) responds with a prayer for God to bless the king.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:1–PSA 72:20

## Psalms 69:4 (id: 24801)

John applied this lament to Jesus ([John 15:25](https://ref.ly/John15:25)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:4

## Psalms 69:5–6 (id: 24802)

The psalmist confesses his sins and prays that God’s response to him will encourage others in similar conditions to place their hope in the Lord.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:5–PSA 69:6

## Psalms 69:7–12 (id: 24803)

Mistreated and abandoned by family, friends, and community, the psalmist turns to the Lord for comfort, relief ([69:13–18](https://ref.ly/Ps69:13-Ps69:18)), and redress ([69:22–28](https://ref.ly/Ps69:22-Ps69:28)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:7–PSA 69:12

## Psalms 69:9 (id: 24804)

Though the psalmist concerns himself with God and all that is holy, insults have been his earthly reward. This verse was later applied to Jesus’ anger at the Temple’s money changers ([John 2:17](https://ref.ly/John2:17)) and to his undeserved suffering ([Rom 15:3](https://ref.ly/Rom15:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:9

## Psalms 69:10–12 (id: 24788)

When the psalmist denies himself normal comforts to seek God in prayer, the wicked ridicule him.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:10–PSA 69:12

## Psalms 69:13–18 (id: 24789)

The psalmist’s only recourse is to pray for rescue and relief from his enemies.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:13–PSA 69:18

## Psalms 69:19–21 (id: 24790)

Alone and misunderstood, the psalmist confesses that he cannot bear his doubts. His heart has been broken by his problems.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:19–PSA 69:21

## Psalms 69:21 (id: 24792)

sour wine for my thirst: This description applies to Jesus’ suffering ([Matt 27:34](https://ref.ly/Matt27:34), [48](https://ref.ly/Matt27:48); [Luke 23:36](https://ref.ly/Luke23:36); [John 19:28–29](https://ref.ly/John19:28-John19:29)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:21

## Psalms 69:22–23 (id: 24793)

The psalmist wants God to transfer his suffering ([69:3](https://ref.ly/Ps69:3)) to his enemies. Paul applied these words to the unbelieving Jews of his day ([Rom 11:9–10](https://ref.ly/Rom11:9-Rom11:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:22–PSA 69:23

## Psalms 69:25 (id: 24794)

Peter quoted this verse after the death of Judas Iscariot ([Acts 1:20](https://ref.ly/Acts1:20)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:25

## Psalms 69:28 (id: 24795)

Erase their names from the Book of Life: Cp. [1:5–6](https://ref.ly/Ps1:5-Ps1:6); [9:5](https://ref.ly/Ps9:5); [Rev 3:5](https://ref.ly/Rev3:5); [20:15](https://ref.ly/Rev20:15); [21:22–27](https://ref.ly/Rev21:22-Rev21:27).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:28

## Psalms 69:29–33 (id: 24796)

The psalmist prays that his pain will turn to praise.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:29–PSA 69:33

## Psalms 69:31 (id: 24797)

Making a vow often included a sacrifice ([20:3](https://ref.ly/Ps20:3); [51:19](https://ref.ly/Ps51:19); cp. [50:13–14](https://ref.ly/Ps50:13-Ps50:14); [51:16](https://ref.ly/Ps51:16)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:31

## Psalms 69:32–33 (id: 24798)

despise: See [22:24](https://ref.ly/Ps22:24); cp. [Matt 25:36](https://ref.ly/Matt25:36); [Heb 13:3](https://ref.ly/Heb13:3).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:32–PSA 69:33

## Psalms 69:34–36 (id: 24799)

The psalmist prays for Zion, offering a new petition from a new circumstance. He applies the prayer ([69:1–33](https://ref.ly/Ps69:1-Ps69:33)) to the desperate condition of Judah (see [51:18–19](https://ref.ly/Ps51:18-Ps51:19)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:34–PSA 69:36

## Psalms 69:36 (id: 24800)

God’s people will inherit the land because the wicked will perish (see [37:8–9](https://ref.ly/Ps37:8-Ps37:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 69:36

## Psalms 70:1 (id: 24822)

The meaning of this phrase is unknown (also found in [38:title](https://ref.ly/Ps38:1)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 70:1

## Psalms 70:1–5 (id: 24821)

This psalm, nearly identical to [40:13–17](https://ref.ly/Ps40:13-Ps40:17), contains an urgency ([70:1](https://ref.ly/Ps70:1), [5](https://ref.ly/Ps70:5)) that fits with the surrounding psalms (cp. [69:17](https://ref.ly/Ps69:17); [71:12](https://ref.ly/Ps71:12)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 70:1–PSA 70:5

## Psalms 71:1–4 (id: 24824)

The poet has learned to acknowledge God as the reliable rock who will do what is right toward him and his oppressors. See also [31:1–3](https://ref.ly/Ps31:1-Ps31:3).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 71:1–PSA 71:4

## Psalms 71:1–24 (id: 24823)

This untitled lament by an aging believer is more of a confession of confidence and hope than the cry of someone abandoned by God. The psalmist experienced God’s power and protection in his youth ([71:5–8](https://ref.ly/Ps71:5-Ps71:8)) and now prays for rescue from the adversaries who stalk him in his old age ([71:9–13](https://ref.ly/Ps71:9-Ps71:13)). Even in the midst of his peril, he continues to tell of God’s past faithfulness and looks forward with hope to a time when he can testify to the next generation that God is faithful and righteous.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 71:1–PSA 71:24

## Psalms 71:5–6 (id: 24831)

The psalmist has trusted God throughout his life (see [22:9](https://ref.ly/Ps22:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 71:5–PSA 71:6

## Psalms 71:9 (id: 24833)

The poet prays for continued fellowship with God (see [9:10](https://ref.ly/Ps9:10); [51:11](https://ref.ly/Ps51:11)). In his weakness, he needs the Lord’s strength even more (see [143:7](https://ref.ly/Ps143:7)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 71:9

## Psalms 71:9–18 (id: 24832)

The aging believer turns to the Lord for hope. As questions from his adversaries get under his skin and his own strength fails, he asks God to answer his prayers. He does not seek answers to prayer for himself but so that he can tell the story to the next generation.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 71:9–PSA 71:18

## Psalms 71:10 (id: 24825)

In their plotting*,* the psalmist’s enemies take counsel and scheme together ([2:2](https://ref.ly/Ps2:2); [21:11](https://ref.ly/Ps21:11); [31:13](https://ref.ly/Ps31:13); [35:4](https://ref.ly/Ps35:4); [63:9–10](https://ref.ly/Ps63:9-Ps63:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 71:10

## Psalms 71:14 (id: 24826)

The psalmist contrasts his own goodness with his enemies’ evil; he centers his life in the Lord, even during hard times (see [9:17–18](https://ref.ly/Ps9:17-Ps9:18); [27:13–14](https://ref.ly/Ps27:13-Ps27:14)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 71:14

## Psalms 71:19–21 (id: 24827)

God’s righteous character provides encouragement and strength in the midst of suffering. The wounded psalmist confesses faith in God’s ability to transform his misery and weakness into abundant life.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 71:19–PSA 71:21

## Psalms 71:20 (id: 24828)

lift me up from the depths: The psalmist hopes for the renewal of an abundant earthly life. This later became grounds for belief in the resurrection of the dead.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 71:20

## Psalms 71:21 (id: 24829)

Being assured of God’s comfort also assures the poet of God’s presence, care, and goodness ([23:4](https://ref.ly/Ps23:4), [6](https://ref.ly/Ps23:6); [86:17](https://ref.ly/Ps86:17)). God’s comfort enhances the psalmist’s sense of being alive (see [119:50](https://ref.ly/Ps119:50)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 71:21

## Psalms 71:22–24 (id: 24830)

As he awaits God’s rescue, the psalmist prepares himself to declare with instruments and voice that God is faithful and righteous.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 71:22–PSA 71:24

## Psalms 72:1 (id: 24845)

love of justice . . . righteousness: Kings in the ancient Near East were expected to represent justice; the standard for Israel’s kings was much higher because the Lord was their model.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 72:1

## Psalms 72:1–7 (id: 24835)

The blessings of justice create a balance in nature, resulting in harmony, prosperity, and fertility.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 72:1–PSA 72:7

## Psalms 72:1–20 (id: 24834)

This royal psalm closes Book Two, with [72:18–20](https://ref.ly/Ps72:18-Ps72:20) functioning as an epilogue to all of Book Two. The psalmist reflects on the prospects of David’s royal line and on Zion (see [Pss 46](https://ref.ly/Ps46:1-Ps46:11), [48](https://ref.ly/Ps48:1-Ps48:14)). He prays that Israel’s kings will be good and prosperous, extending the Lord’s blessing on his people throughout the whole earth. The surpassing righteousness and dominion sought in this prayer foreshadow the coming of Jesus, the Son of David.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 72:1–PSA 72:20

## Psalms 72:3 (id: 24847)

The administration of justice transforms the earth into fruitfulness, abundance, harmony, and goodness (see [Isa 32:16–17](https://ref.ly/Isa32:16-Isa32:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 72:3

## Psalms 72:4 (id: 24848)

A just ruler will defend the poor against injustice, rescue them from violence, and bring their oppressors to account for their wrongdoing.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 72:4

## Psalms 72:5 (id: 24849)

May they fear you: In Hebrew, this clause seems to be a response to the enforcement of justice. The Greek version reads *May they endure,* which makes this a prayer for longevity ([21:4](https://ref.ly/Ps21:4); [72:15](https://ref.ly/Ps72:15), [17](https://ref.ly/Ps72:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 72:5

## Psalms 72:6–7 (id: 24850)

The administration of justice is refreshing like spring rain*,* enabling the godly to flourish (see [Isa 45:8](https://ref.ly/Isa45:8)) like flowers. • until the moon is no more: This phrase means forever (see [Ps 89:36–37](https://ref.ly/Ps89:36-Ps89:37)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 72:6–PSA 72:7

## Psalms 72:8–9 (id: 24852)

from sea to sea: This phrase represents the whole world. • Solomon’s kingdom extended from the Euphrates River in the east to the Philistine territory in the west ([1 Kgs 4:21](https://ref.ly/1Kgs4:21); see [Pss 80:8–11](https://ref.ly/Ps80:8-Ps80:11); [89:19–25](https://ref.ly/Ps89:19-Ps89:25)), where the Mediterranean Sea appeared to be at the ends of the earth (see [2:8](https://ref.ly/Ps2:8)). Everyone will bow before this king, including the desert nomads ([72:9](https://ref.ly/Ps72:9)), his enemies*,* and all the kings of the world (see [Mic 7:17](https://ref.ly/Mic7:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 72:8–PSA 72:9

## Psalms 72:8–11 (id: 24851)

The anointed king will rule over the whole world. Even hostile nations will submit to him. In the ancient Near East, bearing gifts to the king acknowledged his success and his reputation (see [1 Kgs 10](https://ref.ly/1Kgs10:1-1Kgs10:29)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 72:8–PSA 72:11

## Psalms 72:10 (id: 24836)

The western kings of Tarshish came from a major colony in the western basin of the Mediterranean. The eastern kings of Sheba came from an area in southern Arabia, home of the queen of Sheba ([1 Kgs 10](https://ref.ly/1Kgs10:1-1Kgs10:29)). • Seba was probably located somewhere south of Egypt, perhaps on the western shore of the Red Sea across from Sheba. • The gifts offered to the king represented a tribute and a token of submission.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 72:10

## Psalms 72:11 (id: 24837)

All kings and all nations must submit to the messianic king. The hope expressed in [2:10–11](https://ref.ly/Ps2:10-Ps2:11) will be realized (see [96:1–3](https://ref.ly/Ps96:1-Ps96:3); [1 Cor 15:25](https://ref.ly/1Cor15:25)) when other leaders and peoples bow before and serve this king.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 72:11

## Psalms 72:12 (id: 24839)

To rescue the poor means to bring an end to the rule of their oppressors.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 72:12

## Psalms 72:12–14 (id: 24838)

The anointed king will rescue the needy. He is not only the judge but also a father to the poor ([10:14](https://ref.ly/Ps10:14), [17](https://ref.ly/Ps10:17); [68:5](https://ref.ly/Ps68:5)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 72:12–PSA 72:14

## Psalms 72:13 (id: 24840)

Like a father, the king feels pity for those in need (see [Mal 3:17](https://ref.ly/Mal3:17)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 72:13

## Psalms 72:15 (id: 24842)

The people will bless him*,* fulfilling God’s promise to Abraham ([Gen 12:3](https://ref.ly/Gen12:3); see [Ps 133:3](https://ref.ly/Ps133:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 72:15

## Psalms 72:15–17 (id: 24841)

The just kingdom will be long\-lasting and universal. The summary combines the themes of abundance of crops, longevity, tribute of the nations, and the prayer of the nations.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 72:15–PSA 72:17

## Psalms 72:16 (id: 24843)

the trees of Lebanon: Lebanon was renowned for its lumber (see study note on 2 Chr 2:8).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 72:16
* **Associated Articles:** 2 Chronicles 2:8 (ID: `14067@TyndaleStudyNotes`)

## Psalms 72:18–19 (id: 24844)

This doxology concludes Book Two of the Psalter.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 72:18–PSA 72:19

## Psalms 72:20 (id: 24846)

This parenthetical note ends Book Two, which consists mostly of prayers of David. Books Three to Five were probably compiled after Book Two; they include additional psalms of David ([Pss 86](https://ref.ly/Ps86:1-Ps86:17), [101](https://ref.ly/Ps101:1-Ps101:8), [103](https://ref.ly/Ps103:1-Ps103:22), [108–110](https://ref.ly/Ps108:1-Ps110:7), [122](https://ref.ly/Ps122:1-Ps122:9), [124](https://ref.ly/Ps124:1-Ps124:8), [131](https://ref.ly/Ps131:1-Ps131:3), [133](https://ref.ly/Ps133:1-Ps133:3), [138–145](https://ref.ly/Ps138:1-Ps145:21)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 72:20

## Psalms 73:1 (id: 24859)

People whose hearts are pure are renewed by God’s Spirit ([51:10](https://ref.ly/Ps51:10)), commit themselves to lives of godliness, keep away from anything that is sinful, and enjoy God’s presence (see [24:3–4](https://ref.ly/Ps24:3-Ps24:4)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 73:1

## Psalms 73:1–28 (id: 24853)

This wisdom psalm examines the injustice of the prosperity of the wicked. The psalmist affirms that God is good to the godly but his own experience differs ([73:2–12](https://ref.ly/Ps73:2-Ps73:12)). Nearly overcome by his doubts ([73:13–16](https://ref.ly/Ps73:13-Ps73:16)), the psalmist meets the Lord in the sanctuary and gains a perspective that stretches beyond his life and renews his confidence in God ([73:17–26](https://ref.ly/Ps73:17-Ps73:26)). His disturbing doubts stir a greater passion for truth. He knows that he can trust God and that God will rescue him ([73:27–28](https://ref.ly/Ps73:27-Ps73:28)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 73:1–PSA 73:28

## Psalms 73:title (id: 24867)

was a Levitical singer appointed by David ([1 Chr 6:39](https://ref.ly/1Chr6:39)); his descendants were singers and instrumentalists ([1 Chr 15:16–17](https://ref.ly/1Chr15:16-1Chr15:17)). Many of the psalms of Asaph ([Pss 50](https://ref.ly/Ps50:1-Ps50:23), [73–83](https://ref.ly/Ps73:1-Ps83:18)) were probably written by Asaph’s descendants at a later time in Israel’s history (e.g., [Ps 74](https://ref.ly/Ps74:1-Ps74:23)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 73:1

## Psalms 73:1–75.10 (id: 24854)

The apparent prosperity of the wicked ([Ps 73](https://ref.ly/Ps73:1-Ps73:28)) and God’s apparent rejection of his people ([Ps 74](https://ref.ly/Ps74:1-Ps74:23)) raise questions about his justice. God is sovereign over the whole world, and he determines the time for judgment ([Ps 75](https://ref.ly/Ps75:1-Ps75:10)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 73:1–PSA 75:10

## Psalms 73:2–3 (id: 24860)

The poet sees the prosperity of the wicked as unfair (see [72:7](https://ref.ly/Ps72:7)) and resents it. • The psalmist’s feet were slipping from the way of wisdom and godliness (see [17:5](https://ref.ly/Ps17:5); [37:31](https://ref.ly/Ps37:31)), but God kept him from falling off the trail entirely ([38:16](https://ref.ly/Ps38:16); [66:9](https://ref.ly/Ps66:9); [94:18](https://ref.ly/Ps94:18); [121:3](https://ref.ly/Ps121:3)). • when I saw them prosper: Cp. [72:3](https://ref.ly/Ps72:3), [7](https://ref.ly/Ps72:7).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 73:2–PSA 73:3

## Psalms 73:4–12 (id: 24864)

The psalmist presents a caricature of the bliss of the wicked (cp. [Ps 1](https://ref.ly/Ps1:1-Ps1:6)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 73:4–PSA 73:12

## Psalms 73:6 (id: 24865)

The proud adorn themselves with pride and cruelty rather than wisdom ([Prov 1:9](https://ref.ly/Prov1:9)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 73:6

## Psalms 73:9 (id: 24866)

The proud boast, claiming that they own everything and are not subject to anyone (see [2:1–3](https://ref.ly/Ps2:1-Ps2:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 73:9

## Psalms 73:13–14 (id: 24855)

These rhetorical questions expect a discouraging “yes” for an answer. The psalmist wonders if godliness has become a meaningless ritual (see [Mal 3:14–15](https://ref.ly/Mal3:14-Mal3:15)). • My heart refers to his whole being. • The psalmist’s pain comes from discipline and rebuke ([Ps 39:11](https://ref.ly/Ps39:11); [Prov 1:23](https://ref.ly/Prov1:23), [25](https://ref.ly/Prov1:25); [12:1](https://ref.ly/Prov12:1)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 73:13–PSA 73:14

## Psalms 73:13–17 (id: 24856)

Recognizing the ramifications of his discouragement and doubt, the psalmist turns to the Lord and receives special insight.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 73:13–PSA 73:17

## Psalms 73:15–16 (id: 24857)

If the psalmist had verbalized his suspicions about the futility of godly living, he would have discouraged God’s people and given God’s enemies an opportunity to blaspheme (cp. [John 13:20](https://ref.ly/John13:20)). Instead, the psalmist internalizes the problem and reflects on it.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 73:15–PSA 73:16

## Psalms 73:17–20 (id: 24858)

At this time, God’s sanctuary was the Temple in Jerusalem. There, in God’s presence, the psalmist receives special insight (cp. [18:6](https://ref.ly/Ps18:6)). • God deals with the wicked over the long term. He waits patiently and gives them time to repent ([Ezek 33:11](https://ref.ly/Ezek33:11); [2 Pet 3:9](https://ref.ly/2Pet3:9)); those who do not repent will get what they deserve.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 73:17–PSA 73:20

## Psalms 73:21–22 (id: 24861)

The psalmist’s feelings about the prosperity of the wicked had given him a bitter spirit.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 73:21–PSA 73:22

## Psalms 73:24 (id: 24862)

The Lord leads the godly through their troubles and changes their misery into splendor.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 73:24

## Psalms 73:25–26 (id: 24863)

Nothing in heaven or on earth is better than being near God.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 73:25–PSA 73:26

## Psalms 74:1 (id: 24874)

God rejected his people because their sin aroused his anger ([106:34–46](https://ref.ly/Ps106:34-Ps106:46)). • Like a tender shepherd, God cares for his people, the sheep of his pasture ([Pss 23](https://ref.ly/Ps23:1-Ps23:6); [100:3](https://ref.ly/Ps100:3)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 74:1

## Psalms 74:1–2 (id: 24869)

In the aftermath of Jerusalem’s fall, it seemed that God had forgotten his special covenant relationship with his people.

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 74:1–PSA 74:2

## Psalms 74:1–23 (id: 24868)

The psalmist laments the destruction of the Temple in Jerusalem (586 BC) with vivid imagery, questions, fresh memories, and a direct appeal for the Lord to act ([74:19–23](https://ref.ly/Ps74:19-Ps74:23)).

* **Associated Passages:** PSA 74:1–PSA 74:23

## Psalms 74:2 (id: 24875)

The Lord chose and redeemed Israel from Egypt in the Exodus (cp.
